<p>The <b>Rose Bowl Game</b> is an annual American <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a> <a href="/wiki/Bowl_game" title="Bowl game">bowl game</a>, usually played on January 1 (New Year's Day) at the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_(stadium)" title="Rose Bowl (stadium)">Rose Bowl</a> stadium in <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">Pasadena, California</a>. When New Year's Day falls on a Sunday, the game is played on Monday, January 2 (15 times now). The Rose Bowl is nicknamed "<b>The Granddaddy of Them All</b>" because it is the oldest <a href="/wiki/Bowl_game" title="Bowl game">bowl game</a>. It was first played in 1902, and has been played annually since 1916. Since 1945, it has been the highest attended college football bowl game.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> It is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Pasadena_Tournament_of_Roses_Association" title="Pasadena Tournament of Roses Association">Pasadena Tournament of Roses Association</a>'s "America's New Year Celebration," which also includes the historic <a href="/wiki/Rose_Parade" title="Rose Parade">Rose Parade</a>.</p>
<p>Beginning with the 2015 game, the official name for sponsorship reasons is the "Rose Bowl Game presented by <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Mutual" title="Northwestern Mutual">Northwestern Mutual</a>". <a href="/wiki/Vizio" title="Vizio">Vizio</a> was the previous sponsor from 2012; prior sponsors included <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citi" class="mw-redirect" title="Citi">Citi</a>. In 2015, the game was also officially known as the "College Football Playoff semifinal at the Rose Bowl Game presented by Northwestern Mutual."</p>
<p>The Rose Bowl Game has traditionally hosted the conference champions from the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pac-12_Conference" title="Pac-12 Conference">Pac-12</a> conferences (or their predecessors), but due to its past and present membership in two consortia that seek to determine a <a href="/wiki/College_football_national_championships_in_NCAA_Division_I_FBS" title="College football national championships in NCAA Division I FBS">national champion in Division I FBS</a>, in 2002, the Rose Bowl began to infrequently deviate from its traditional match-up in order to facilitate championship games. In 2002 and 2006 (2001 and 2005 football seasons), under the <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">Bowl Championship Series</a> system, the Rose Bowl was designated as its championship game, and hosted the top two teams determined by the BCS system. Beginning in <a href="/wiki/2015_Rose_Bowl" title="2015 Rose Bowl">2015</a>, the Rose Bowl has been part of the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Playoff" title="College Football Playoff">College Football Playoff</a> system and hosts one of its semi-final games every three years. During non-Playoff years, the Rose Bowl reverts to a Pac-12/Big Ten matchup.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Originally titled the "Tournament East–West football game",<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/1902_Rose_Bowl" title="1902 Rose Bowl">first Rose Bowl</a> was played on January 1, 1902, starting the tradition of New Year's Day bowl games. The football game was added in 1902 to help fund the cost of the Rose Parade.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> The inaugural game featured <a href="/wiki/Fielding_H._Yost" title="Fielding H. Yost">Fielding H. Yost</a>'s dominating <a href="/wiki/1901_Michigan_Wolverines_football_team" title="1901 Michigan Wolverines football team">1901 Michigan</a> team, representing the East, which crushed a previously 3-1-2 team from <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, representing the West, by a score of 49–0 after Stanford quit in the third quarter. Michigan finished the season 11–0 and was crowned the national champion. Yost had been Stanford's coach the previous year. The game was so lopsided that for the next thirteen years, the Tournament of Roses officials ran chariot races, ostrich races, and other various events instead of football.<sup id="cite_ref-Ours_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ours-7">[6]</a></sup> But, on New Year's Day <a href="/wiki/1916_Rose_Bowl" title="1916 Rose Bowl">1916</a>, football returned to stay as the <a href="/wiki/1915_Washington_State_Cougars_football_team" class="mw-redirect" title="1915 Washington State Cougars football team">State College of Washington (now Washington State University)</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/1915_Brown_Bruins_football_team" class="mw-redirect" title="1915 Brown Bruins football team">Brown University</a> in the first annual Rose Bowl with that explicit name.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tournament_Park_and_Rose_Bowl_stadium">Tournament Park and Rose Bowl stadium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Tournament Park and Rose Bowl stadium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_(stadium)" title="Rose Bowl (stadium)">Rose Bowl</a> was built for the January 1, 1923 match, games were played in Pasadena's <a href="/wiki/Tournament_Park" title="Tournament Park">Tournament Park</a>, approximately three miles (5&#160;km) southeast of the current Rose Bowl stadium near the campus of <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a>. Tournament Park was determined to be unsuitable for the larger and larger crowds gathering to watch the game and a new, permanent home for the game was commissioned.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:1st-Rose-Bowl-game-1902.jpg" class="image" title="The very first Rose Bowl Game at Tournament park in 1902"><img alt="The very first Rose Bowl Game at Tournament park in 1902" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/1st-Rose-Bowl-game-1902.jpg" width="720" height="186" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="186" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The Rose Bowl stadium, designed after the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bowl" title="Yale Bowl">Yale Bowl</a> in New Haven, then hosted the first "Rose Bowl" game in 1923. The name of the stadium was alternatively "Tournament of Roses Stadium" or "Tournament of Roses Bowl", until the name "Rose Bowl" was settled on before the <a href="/wiki/1923_Rose_Bowl" title="1923 Rose Bowl">1923</a> game.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The stadium seating has been reconfigured several times since its original construction in 1922. For many years, the Rose Bowl stadium had the largest football stadium capacity in the United States, eventually being surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Stadium" title="Michigan Stadium">Michigan Stadium</a> in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> The maximum stated <a href="/wiki/Seating_capacity" title="Seating capacity">seating capacity</a> was 104,594 from 1972 to 1997. Capacity was lowered following <a href="/wiki/1998_Rose_Bowl" title="1998 Rose Bowl">1998</a>; the <a href="/wiki/2006_Rose_Bowl" title="2006 Rose Bowl">2006</a> game, which was also the BCS championship game, had a crowd of 93,986; and a crowd of 94,118 saw the <a href="/wiki/2011_Rose_Bowl" title="2011 Rose Bowl">2011</a> game between TCU and Wisconsin.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> As of 2012, the Rose Bowl is number seven on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_football_stadiums_by_capacity" title="List of American football stadiums by capacity">List of American football stadiums by capacity</a> with a current official <a href="/wiki/Seating_capacity" title="Seating capacity">seating capacity</a> of 92,542 and is still the largest stadium that hosts post-season bowl games.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> The Rose Bowl is also the only BCS bowl game that is held in a non-NFL stadium.</p>
<h3><span id="Team_selection_1916–1946"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_selection_1916.E2.80.931946">Team selection 1916–1946</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Team selection 1916–1946">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the game’s early years, except during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the Rose Bowl always pitted a team—not necessarily the conference champion—from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Conference" title="Pacific Coast Conference">Pacific Coast Conference</a> (PCC), the predecessor of the current <a href="/wiki/Pac-12_Conference" title="Pac-12 Conference">Pac-12 Conference</a>, against an opponent from the Eastern U.S. During the last two years of World War I, teams from military bases met in the Rose Bowl. During its history, a number of notable matchups have been made with the top football teams and top coaches of the time. These include the <a href="/wiki/1925_Rose_Bowl" title="1925 Rose Bowl">1925</a> game, with <a href="/wiki/Knute_Rockne" title="Knute Rockne">Knute Rockne</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=1924_Notre_Dame_Fighitng_Irish_football_team&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1924 Notre Dame Fighitng Irish football team (page does not exist)">Notre Dame</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Four_Horsemen_(football)" class="mw-redirect" title="Four Horsemen (football)">Four Horsemen</a>, against <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Scobey_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Glenn Scobey Warner">"Pop" Warner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1924_Stanford_football_team" title="1924 Stanford football team">Stanford</a>; the <a href="/wiki/1926_Rose_Bowl" title="1926 Rose Bowl">1926</a> edition saw the <a href="/wiki/1925_Alabama_Crimson_Tide_football_team" title="1925 Alabama Crimson Tide football team">Alabama Crimson Tide</a>’s win over <a href="/wiki/1925_Washington_Huskies_football_team" title="1925 Washington Huskies football team">Washington</a>; and <a href="/wiki/1940_Rose_Bowl" title="1940 Rose Bowl">1940</a> featured <a href="/wiki/Howard_Jones_(American_football_coach)" title="Howard Jones (American football coach)">Howard Jones</a>' <a href="/wiki/1939_USC_Trojans_football_team" title="1939 USC Trojans football team">USC Trojans</a> against <a href="/wiki/Bob_Neyland" class="mw-redirect" title="Bob Neyland">Bob Neyland</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1939_Tennessee_Volunteers_football_team" title="1939 Tennessee Volunteers football team">Tennessee Volunteers</a>. During this period, there were ten games in which undefeated teams were matched.</p>
<h3><span id="World_War_II_-_1942_venue_change_to_Durham,_North_Carolina"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_-_1942_venue_change_to_Durham.2C_North_Carolina">World War II - 1942 venue change to Durham, North Carolina</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: World War II - 1942 venue change to Durham, North Carolina">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/1942_Rose_Bowl" title="1942 Rose Bowl">1942 Rose Bowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/1943_Rose_Bowl" title="1943 Rose Bowl">1943 Rose Bowl</a></div>
<p>After the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a> on December 7, 1941, and a series of attacks on West Coast shipping beginning on December 18,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> there were concerns about a possible Japanese attack on the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">West Coast</a>. The Rose Parade, with a million watchers, and the Rose Bowl, with 90,000 spectators, were presumed to be ideal targets for the Japanese. Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/John_L._DeWitt" title="John L. DeWitt">John L. DeWitt</a> recommended that the Rose Parade and Rose Bowl festivities be canceled.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The Rose Bowl committee originally planned to cancel the game. On December 16, Duke University invited the game and Oregon State to Duke's home stadium in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_North_Carolina" title="Durham, North Carolina">Durham, North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rose_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rose-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> After the 1942 Allied victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">Battle of Midway</a> and the end of the Japanese offensives in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific Theater</a> during 1942, it was deemed that the West Coast was no longer vulnerable to attack, and the Rose Bowl game continued on in the Rose Bowl Stadium. Few Georgia fans were able to make the trip to the 1943 Rose Bowl because of wartime travel restrictions.<sup id="cite_ref-LEOCOSTA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LEOCOSTA-19">[18]</a></sup> There were a large number of military servicemen in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tournament_of_Roses_parade" class="mw-redirect" title="Tournament of Roses parade">Tournament of Roses parade</a> itself still was not held in 1943 due to the war.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Big_Nine–PCC_agreement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Nine.E2.80.93PCC_agreement">Big Nine–PCC agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Big Nine–PCC agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, many college football schools had dropped some conference opponents and instead played football against local military base teams. Many colleges could not even field teams due to the draft and manpower requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> After the war was over, demobilization and the <a href="/wiki/G.I._Bill" title="G.I. Bill">G.I. Bill</a> enabled returning servicemen to attend college. The 1946 season was the first true post-war college football season with travel restrictions lifted and civilian college opponents returning to schedules.</p>
<p>The Big Nine and PCC were of the same accord when it came to treating players as amateurs, as compared to the semi-professional status that the Southern Universities proposed. Also, the Big Nine and PCC both had the same attitudes towards desegregation and allowing African-Americans to play football.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Many other universities were still segregated. None of the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> schools had an African American athlete until 1966. The Cotton Bowl, Orange Bowl, and Sugar Bowl would not be integrated until 1948, 1955, and 1956 respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Big Nine agreed in 1946, after eight years of negotiating over payments, rules, and ticket allocations, to a five-year exclusive deal with the Rose Bowl to send the conference champion to meet the PCC conference champion.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> UCLA, USC, Minnesota and Illinois all voted against it.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1947_Rose_Bowl" title="1947 Rose Bowl">1947 Rose Bowl</a>, with UCLA meeting Illinios, was the first game under this agreement.</p>
<h3><span id="Big_Ten–AAWU/Pac-8/10_agreement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Ten.E2.80.93AAWU.2FPac-8.2F10_agreement">Big Ten–AAWU/Pac-8/10 agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Big Ten–AAWU/Pac-8/10 agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When the PCC dissolved prior to the 1959 season following a pay-for-play scandal in 1958, there was no official agreement in force. The Tournament of Roses selected from the former members of PCC and invited <a href="/wiki/1959_Washington_Huskies_football_team" title="1959 Washington Huskies football team">Washington</a>, the first champion of the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Athletic_Association_of_Western_Universities" class="mw-redirect" title="Athletic Association of Western Universities">Athletic Association of Western Universities</a> (AAWU), to play Big Ten champion <a href="/wiki/1959_Wisconsin_Badgers_football_team" title="1959 Wisconsin Badgers football team">Wisconsin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1960_Rose_Bowl" title="1960 Rose Bowl">1960 Rose Bowl</a>. The Big Ten authorized its members to accept any Rose Bowl invitation at their discretion.</p>
<p>The AAWU signed an agreement with the Rose Bowl that remained in force from the <a href="/wiki/1961_Rose_Bowl" title="1961 Rose Bowl">1961 Rose Bowl</a> until the advent of the BCS era in 1998. In 1962, after Minnesota changed its vote against pursuing a new agreement (resolving a 5–5 voting deadlock which had prevented any new negotiations for years), a Big Ten agreement was finalized, which went into effect with the <a href="/wiki/1963_Rose_Bowl" title="1963 Rose Bowl">1963 Rose Bowl</a> and lasted until the BCS era.</p>
<p>While the Big Ten supplied the "East" representative and the PCC, AAWU, or Pac-8/10 supplied the "West" representative from the <a href="/wiki/1947_Rose_Bowl" title="1947 Rose Bowl">1947 Rose Bowl</a> to the BCS era, statements about an "exclusive" Rose Bowl agreement existing during this period are not entirely accurate: the Big Ten was not part of any agreement for the 1961 and 1962 games and the status of the agreement for 1960 is questionable, at best. The fact that the 1961 Big Ten champion, <a href="/wiki/1961_Ohio_State_Buckeyes_football_team" title="1961 Ohio State Buckeyes football team">Ohio State</a>, declined the invitation to play in the <a href="/wiki/1962_Rose_Bowl" title="1962 Rose Bowl">1962 Rose Bowl</a> (without penalty) is the clearest evidence that this "exclusive agreement" did not exist in these years.</p>
<p>The AAWU used "Big Five", "Big Six", and "Pacific-8" as unofficial nicknames (each reflecting the number of conference members). It officially adopted the "Pacific-8" name for the 1968 season. The name changed to "Pacific-10" with the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Wildcats" title="Arizona Wildcats">Arizona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona_State_Sun_Devils" title="Arizona State Sun Devils">Arizona State</a> in 1978, its last official name change prior to the formation of the BCS in 1998. The Big Ten Conference retained the same name throughout this period, even though it had eleven members by the start of the BCS era due to the addition of <a href="/wiki/Penn_State_Nittany_Lions" title="Penn State Nittany Lions">Penn State</a> in 1990.</p>
<p>Both conferences had a "no repeat" rule in force for a number of years. Under this rule, any team that had appeared in the Rose Bowl game the previous season could not go, even if they were the conference champion. The notable exceptions to this rule were Washington playing in the 1960 and 1961 games and Minnesota playing in the 1961 and 1962 games during the period when the conference agreements were in a state of flux. The Big Ten abolished this rule in 1972. The AAWU/Pac-8 had abandoned its no-repeat rule by the time Southern California played in four consecutive Rose Bowl games from 1967 to 1970.</p>
<p>Both conferences also had "exclusive agreements" with the Rose Bowl game, in the sense that member schools were not allowed to play in any other bowl game. Both conferences abolished this rule before the 1975 college football season. As a result, <a href="/wiki/1975_Michigan_Wolverines_football_team" title="1975 Michigan Wolverines football team">Michigan</a> and <a href="/wiki/1975_USC_Trojans_football_team" title="1975 USC Trojans football team">USC</a> were allowed to play in the <a href="/wiki/1976_Orange_Bowl" title="1976 Orange Bowl">1976 Orange Bowl</a> and the 1975 <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Bowl" title="Liberty Bowl">Liberty Bowl</a>, respectively.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bowl_Championship_Series">Bowl Championship Series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bowl Championship Series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<p>As of the <a href="/wiki/1998_NCAA_Division_I-A_football_season" title="1998 NCAA Division I-A football season">1998 season</a>, with the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">Bowl Championship Series</a> (BCS), team selection for the Rose Bowl was tied to the other three BCS bowls, although in any given year the Rose Bowl still attempted, if possible, to maintain the traditional Pac-10 (Pac-12 after the addition of <a href="/wiki/Utah_Utes" title="Utah Utes">Utah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Buffalos" class="mw-redirect" title="Colorado Buffalos">Colorado</a> in 2011) versus Big Ten format (though if the champ from either or both conference was ranked BCS #1 or #2, they were allowed into the national championship game and the runner up of their conference got the nod). Twice in this era, the Rose Bowl had served as the BCS championship game.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2002_Rose_Bowl" title="2002 Rose Bowl">2002 game</a> served as the BCS championship game between the BCS No. 1–ranked <a href="/wiki/2001_Miami_Hurricanes_football_team" title="2001 Miami Hurricanes football team">Miami</a>, then a member of the <a href="/wiki/Big_East_Conference_(1979%E2%80%932013)" title="Big East Conference (1979–2013)">Big East Conference</a>, and the BCS No. 2–ranked <a href="/wiki/2001_Nebraska_Cornhuskers_football_team" title="2001 Nebraska Cornhuskers football team">Nebraska</a>, then a member of the <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">Big 12 Conference</a>. The Nebraska selection as the BCS No. 2 team was controversial because <a href="/wiki/2001_Oregon_Ducks_football_team" title="2001 Oregon Ducks football team">Oregon</a> was ranked No. 2 in both the AP and Coaches Polls, while Nebraska was ranked No. 4 in both polls and did not play in its conference championship game (No. 3 Colorado, who would play Oregon in that year's <a href="/wiki/Fiesta_Bowl" title="Fiesta Bowl">Fiesta Bowl</a>, did and won the Big 12's automatic bid to the BCS). This prevented a West Coast team playing in the Rose Bowl for their first time, and it also marked the first matchup since 1946 not to feature the traditional pairing of Pac-10 vs. Big Ten teams.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2006_Rose_Bowl" title="2006 Rose Bowl">2006 Rose Bowl</a> game featured offensive powerhouses <a href="/wiki/2005_Texas_Longhorn_football_team" class="mw-redirect" title="2005 Texas Longhorn football team">Texas</a>, riding a 19-game winning streak, and <a href="/wiki/2005_USC_Trojans_football_team" title="2005 USC Trojans football team">USC</a>, which entered the game with a 34-game winning streak and two Heisman Trophy winners. Texas won 41–38. The game had a television viewership of 35.6 million,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> the highest for college football contest since the <a href="/wiki/1987_Fiesta_Bowl" title="1987 Fiesta Bowl">1987 Fiesta Bowl</a> between <a href="/wiki/1986_Penn_State_Nittany_Lions_football_team" title="1986 Penn State Nittany Lions football team">Penn State</a> and <a href="/wiki/1986_Miami_Hurricanes_football_team" title="1986 Miami Hurricanes football team">Miami</a>.</p>
<p>On two other occasions during the BCS era, Rose Bowl participation had expanded beyond the Big Ten and Pac-10. The <a href="/wiki/2003_Rose_Bowl" title="2003 Rose Bowl">2003 Rose Bowl</a> couldn't select Big Ten co-champion and automatic qualifier Ohio State, who finished No. 2 in the BCS and thus received a bid to the <a href="/wiki/2003_Fiesta_Bowl" title="2003 Fiesta Bowl">Fiesta Bowl</a> to play for the national championship. The Rose Bowl was poised to select Big Ten co-champion Iowa as an at-large in order to preserve the traditional Big Ten/Pac-10 match up. However, the <a href="/wiki/2003_Orange_Bowl" title="2003 Orange Bowl">Orange Bowl</a>, which selected ahead of the Rose Bowl that year, chose the Hawkeyes. As a result, the Rose Bowl featured the first appearance by <a href="/wiki/2002_Oklahoma_Sooners_football_team#2002" title="2002 Oklahoma Sooners football team">Oklahoma</a>, who faced Pac-10 Champion <a href="/wiki/Washington_State_Cougars_football" title="Washington State Cougars football">Washington State</a>. The 2005 game featured <a href="/wiki/2004_Texas_Longhorns_football_team" title="2004 Texas Longhorns football team">Texas</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">Big 12 Conference</a>, selected, amid some controversy, over <a href="/wiki/California_Golden_Bears" title="California Golden Bears">California</a> of the Pac-10, marking the second time a West Coast team did not make the Rose Bowl. The controversy was the result of the BCS computer rankings which elevated Texas over California. Texas went on to defeat Michigan in the 2005 game, featuring a four-touchdown performance by <a href="/wiki/Vince_Young" title="Vince Young">Vince Young</a>, foreshadowing his 467-yard performance a year later in the 2006 defeat of USC that won the National Title for Texas.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2004_Rose_Bowl" title="2004 Rose Bowl">2004 game</a> is also noteworthy. In this game, <a href="/wiki/2003_USC_Trojans_football_team" title="2003 USC Trojans football team">USC</a> defeated Michigan, 28–14, thus earning the top ranking in the <a href="/wiki/AP_Poll" title="AP Poll">AP Poll</a> and a share of the national championship with BCS champion <a href="/wiki/2003_LSU_Tigers_football_team" title="2003 LSU Tigers football team">LSU</a>. USC, despite being No. 1 in the AP poll, did not qualify for the BCS championship game because of their standing in the BCS system.</p>
<p>The second BCS-era Rose Bowl arrangement ran from 2004 through 2014. The Big Ten and Pac-12 (the new name of the Pac-10) retained their bids, but a provision was inserted mandating that the first time that either conference could not fill their bid (due to a school from the Big Ten or Pac-12 qualifying for the BCS National Championship Game), and if a non-BCS conference school qualified, the Rose Bowl was required to take that school.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> As a result, Texas Christian University (TCU) became the first team from a non-automatic qualifying conference to play in the Rose Bowl in the BCS era. The <a href="/wiki/2010_TCU_Horned_Frogs_football_team" title="2010 TCU Horned Frogs football team">2010 TCU Horned Frogs</a> finished their second consecutive regular season at 12–0, were back-to-back champions of the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_West_Conference" title="Mountain West Conference">Mountain West Conference</a>, and ranked No. 3 in the final BCS Poll. TCU defeated No. 5 <a href="/wiki/2010_Wisconsin_Badgers_football_team" title="2010 Wisconsin Badgers football team">Wisconsin</a> 21–19 in the <a href="/wiki/2011_Rose_Bowl" title="2011 Rose Bowl">2011 Rose Bowl</a>. TCU's appearance satisfied the 'first time' clause of the current agreement.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2014_Rose_Bowl" title="2014 Rose Bowl">100th Rose Bowl Game</a> featured a traditional pairing of Big Ten champion versus Pac-12 champion, with <a href="/wiki/2013_Michigan_State_Spartans_football_team" title="2013 Michigan State Spartans football team">Michigan State</a> playing against <a href="/wiki/2013_Stanford_Cardinal_football_team" title="2013 Stanford Cardinal football team">Stanford</a> on January 1, 2014. Michigan State won the game, 24–20.</p>
<p>The Bowl Championship Series format ended with the <a href="/wiki/2014_BCS_National_Championship_Game" title="2014 BCS National Championship Game">2014 BCS National Championship Game</a>, played at the Rose Bowl stadium on January 6.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="College_Football_Playoff">College Football Playoff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: College Football Playoff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The BCS was replaced in 2014 by the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Playoff" title="College Football Playoff">College Football Playoff</a>, which selects four teams for two national semifinal games, leading to a championship game. As part of the arrangement, the Rose Bowl game functions as a semifinal playoff game every three years. In years when the Rose Bowl is not hosting a semifinal, it takes the Pac-12 and Big Ten champions, provided they are outside of the top four and not in a semifinal.</p>
<p>The first game under the new arrangement was played on January 1, 2015 and was known as the <i>College Football Playoff semifinal at the Rose Bowl Game presented by Northwestern Mutual</i>. It featured the <a href="/wiki/2014_Oregon_Ducks_football_team" title="2014 Oregon Ducks football team">Oregon Ducks</a> of the Pac-12 Conference and the <a href="/wiki/2014_Florida_State_Seminoles_football_team" title="2014 Florida State Seminoles football team">Florida State Seminoles</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Conference" title="Atlantic Coast Conference">Atlantic Coast Conference</a> team to participate in the Rose Bowl. Oregon defeated Florida State, 59–20, ending the Seminoles' 29-game winning streak, which dated back to the end of the 2012 season. As a result, Oregon advanced to the <a href="/wiki/2015_College_Football_Playoff_National_Championship" title="2015 College Football Playoff National Championship">2015 College Football Playoff National Championship</a> played on January 12. The 59 points were a new Rose Bowl Game scoring record for a team.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2016_Rose_Bowl" title="2016 Rose Bowl">2016 Rose Bowl</a> featured Pac-12 champions Stanford against Big Ten West Division champions Iowa. Stanford defeated Iowa 45–16, scoring 35 points in the first half, the most points ever scored in the first half of a Rose Bowl. Big Ten champions Michigan State defeated Iowa 16–13 in the <a href="/wiki/2015_Big_Ten_Football_Championship_Game" title="2015 Big Ten Football Championship Game">Big Ten championship Game</a>, but lost 38-0 to Alabama in a <a href="/wiki/2015_Cotton_Bowl_Classic_(December)" title="2015 Cotton Bowl Classic (December)">CFP semifinal</a> on New Year's Eve. There was some controversy over the selection of the Big Ten's Rose Bowl representative, given that both Iowa and Ohio State finished their seasons with only one loss, both losing to Michigan State. In the end, the College Football Playoff Committee ranked Iowa ahead of Ohio State, which led to Iowa's first Rose Bowl berth since 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Iowa was ranked fifth in the final College Football Playoff rankings, and Stanford sixth, meaning that the 2016 Rose Bowl featured the strongest matchup that was not part of the College Football Playoff.</p>
<p>The next semifinal to be hosted at the Rose Bowl will be on January 1, 2018.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sponsorship_and_broadcasting_rights">Sponsorship and broadcasting rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sponsorship and broadcasting rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_broadcasters" class="mw-redirect" title="Rose Bowl broadcasters">Rose Bowl broadcasters</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg/220px-CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg/330px-CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg/440px-CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CardStunt-010104-RoseBowl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Large <a href="/wiki/Card_stunt" title="Card stunt">card stunt</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/2004_Rose_Bowl" title="2004 Rose Bowl">2004</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For many years the Rose Bowl eschewed sponsorship, but in <a href="/wiki/1999_Rose_Bowl" title="1999 Rose Bowl">1999</a>, the it became "The Rose Bowl Game presented by <a href="/wiki/American_Telephone_%26_Telegraph_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="American Telephone &amp; Telegraph Company">AT&amp;T</a>." Unlike the other bowl games, the sponsor was not added to the title of the game, but instead as a presenter.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> In 2002 it was branded The Rose Bowl Game presented by <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PlayStation 2</a>. From 2003 to 2010, after the agreement with <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> expired, the game was presented by <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citi</a>.</p>
<p>In June 2010, Citi decided to end sponsorship of the Rose Bowl games, including the National Championship game.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> In October 2010, <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">HDTV</a> maker <a href="/wiki/Vizio" title="Vizio">Vizio</a> signed a 4-year contract to be the official sponsor of the Rose Bowl games through 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> After Vizio declined to renew sponsorship in 2014, financial services giant <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Mutual" title="Northwestern Mutual">Northwestern Mutual</a> became the new presenting sponsor.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1952_Rose_Bowl" title="1952 Rose Bowl">1952</a> game was the first nationally televised bowl game and the first nationally televised college game of any sport.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> From 1952 to <a href="/wiki/1988_Rose_Bowl" title="1988 Rose Bowl">1988</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-idmsc88_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idmsc88-37">[36]</a></sup> the Rose Bowl was televised by <a href="/wiki/NBC_Sports" title="NBC Sports">NBC</a> at 2 p.m. <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">PST</a>, and in most years was the only New Year's Day bowl airing at that time. The <a href="/wiki/1962_Rose_Bowl" title="1962 Rose Bowl">1962</a> game was the first college football game broadcast in color.</p>
<p>From <a href="/wiki/1989_Rose_Bowl" title="1989 Rose Bowl">1989</a> to <a href="/wiki/2010_Rose_Bowl" title="2010 Rose Bowl">2010</a>, the game was broadcast on <a href="/wiki/ESPN_College_Football_on_ABC" title="ESPN College Football on ABC">ABC</a>, usually at 2 p.m. PST; the <a href="/wiki/2005_Rose_Bowl" title="2005 Rose Bowl">2005</a> edition was the first one broadcast in <a href="/wiki/HDTV" class="mw-redirect" title="HDTV">HDTV</a>. Beginning in 2007, <a href="/wiki/Fox_Sports_(USA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox Sports (USA)">FOX</a> had the broadcast rights to the other <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">Bowl Championship Series</a> games, but the Rose Bowl, which negotiates its own television contract independent of the BCS, had agreed to keep the game on ABC.</p>
<p>Beginning with the 2010 season, <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> (majority-owned by ABC's parent company, <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a>) now broadcasts all the BCS/CFP games, including the Rose Bowl game.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> The game is also broadcast nationally by <a href="/wiki/ESPN_Radio" title="ESPN Radio">ESPN Radio</a> and by ESPN International for Latin America. In 2013 <a href="/wiki/ESPN_Deportes" title="ESPN Deportes">ESPN Deportes</a> provided the first Spanish language telecast in the U.S. of the Rose Bowl Game.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Rose Bowl game contract with ESPN was extended on June 28, 2012, to 2026, for a reportedly $80 million per year.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Mutual" title="Northwestern Mutual">Northwestern Mutual</a> took over as presenting sponsor in <a href="/wiki/2015_Rose_Bowl" title="2015 Rose Bowl">2015</a> and will continue until 2020.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequent_participants">Frequent participants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Frequent participants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">If there are any Big Ten teams that shoot for a national championship, they're damn fools...You play to win the Big Ten championship, and if you win it and go to the Rose Bowl and win it, then you've had a great season.</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;Bo Schembechler of Michigan, July 1989<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>USC has played the most times in the Rose Bowl, with 34 appearances, followed by Michigan (20), Stanford (15), Ohio State and Washington (14 each), and UCLA (12). <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Crimson_Tide_football" title="Alabama Crimson Tide football">Alabama</a>, 4–1–1 in Rose Bowls, has made the most appearances of any team outside the Pac-12 and Big Ten conferences. Pittsburgh appeared four times in a nine-year period, from 1927 to 1936. Among Pac-8/10/12 and Big Ten schools, the record for longest drought since a team's last Rose Bowl appearance is held by California (1959), followed by Minnesota (1962), Oregon State (1965), and Indiana (1968).</p>
<p>USC has also won the most Rose Bowls (25), followed by Michigan (8); Washington, Ohio State, and Stanford (7 each); and UCLA (5). Michigan has lost the most (12), followed by USC (9), Ohio State, UCLA and Stanford (7 each), Washington and Wisconsin (6 each). Among Pac-8/10/12 and Big Ten schools who have played in at least one Rose Bowl, the record for the longest period since a win is held jointly by Indiana and Nebraska, who have never won, followed by Washington State (1916), Cal (1939), Oregon State (1942), Northwestern (1949), and Iowa (1959). As of 2016, head coaches <a href="/wiki/Howard_Jones_(American_football_coach)" title="Howard Jones (American football coach)">Howard Jones</a> (5–0) and <a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(American_football_coach)" title="John Robinson (American football coach)">John Robinson</a> (4–0) lead the list of undefeated Rose Bowl records.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most frequent Rose Bowl matchup is USC–Michigan, occurring for the eighth time in 2007, with USC holding a 6–2 advantage (including rare meetings outside the Rose Bowl, USC leads this series 6–4). The next most frequent matchup is USC–Ohio State, occurring for the seventh time in 1985, with USC holding a 4–3 advantage.</p>
<p>From the 1946 season (<a href="/wiki/1947_Rose_Bowl" title="1947 Rose Bowl">1947 Rose Bowl</a>), when the Big Ten-Rose Bowl agreement began, through the 1971 season (<a href="/wiki/1972_Rose_Bowl" title="1972 Rose Bowl">1972 Rose Bowl</a>), the Big Ten did not permit the same team to represent that Conference in the Rose Bowl in consecutive years. There was one exception: Minnesota played in the <a href="/wiki/1961_Rose_Bowl" title="1961 Rose Bowl">1961 Rose Bowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/1962_Rose_Bowl" title="1962 Rose Bowl">1962 Rose Bowl</a> games, as previously explained in the section on the conference agreements after the collapse of the Pacific Coast Conference.</p>
<p>Also of note, during this era Big Ten and Pac-8 teams could play only in the Rose Bowl; this restriction was not lifted until the 1975 season.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archie_Griffin" title="Archie Griffin">Archie Griffin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Ohio State">Ohio State</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Cushing" title="Brian Cushing">Brian Cushing</a> of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">USC</a> are the only players to ever start in four Rose Bowl games. Legendary coach <a href="/wiki/Woody_Hayes" title="Woody Hayes">Woody Hayes</a> led Ohio State to the Rose Bowl from 1973 to 1976, while USC head coach <a href="/wiki/Pete_Carroll" title="Pete Carroll">Pete Carroll</a> led the Trojans to the Rose Bowl from 2006 to 2009.</p>
<p>Current members of the <a href="/wiki/Pac-12_Conference" title="Pac-12 Conference">Pac-12</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten</a> to have not appeared in the Rose Bowl are <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Wildcats_football" title="Arizona Wildcats football">Arizona</a> (who joined the then-Pac-10 in 1978), <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Buffaloes_football" title="Colorado Buffaloes football">Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Utah_Utes_football" title="Utah Utes football">Utah</a> (who both joined the Pac-12 in 2011), and <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Terrapins_football" title="Maryland Terrapins football">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_Scarlet_Knights_football" title="Rutgers Scarlet Knights football">Rutgers</a> (who both joined the Big Ten in 2014), though <a href="/wiki/California_Golden_Bears_football" title="California Golden Bears football">California</a> appeared in the Rose Bowl only as a member of a <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Conference" title="Pacific Coast Conference">predecessor league</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pac-12_Conference" title="Pac-12 Conference">Pac-12</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rosebowlgames_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosebowlgames-49">[45]</a></sup> Similar to Cal, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska_Cornhuskers_football" title="Nebraska Cornhuskers football">Nebraska</a> played in the <a href="/wiki/1941_Rose_Bowl" title="1941 Rose Bowl">1941</a> and <a href="/wiki/2002_Rose_Bowl" title="2002 Rose Bowl">2002</a> games, but was not a member of the Big Ten Conference at these times. The <a href="/wiki/Idaho_Vandals_football" title="Idaho Vandals football">Idaho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montana_Grizzlies_football" title="Montana Grizzlies football">Montana</a>, who were members of the Pacific Coast Conference from 1922 until 1958 and 1950 respectively, never finished near the top in the PCC football standings. Former Big Ten member <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Maroons_football" title="Chicago Maroons football">Chicago</a> withdrew from the league prior to the bowl arrangement being set. Relatively new Pac-12 members <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Buffaloes_football" title="Colorado Buffaloes football">Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Utah_Utes_football" title="Utah Utes football">Utah</a> have yet to appear in one, having joined the Pac-12 on July 1, 2011. The most recent additions to the Big Ten, <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Terrapins_football" title="Maryland Terrapins football">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_Scarlet_Knights_football" title="Rutgers Scarlet Knights football">Rutgers</a>, have yet to appear in one, having joined the Big Ten on July 1, 2014.</p>
<p>USC has played the most Big Ten schools in the Rose Bowl. As of 2016, the only opponents remaining for the Trojans are Iowa, Nebraska, Minnesota, and the two newest Big Ten schools that have yet to appear in the Rose Bowl: Maryland and Rutgers. Michigan has played the most schools in the Pac-12 in the Rose Bowl. The remaining opponents for the Wolverines are Oregon and the three Pac-12 schools that have yet to appear in the Rose Bowl: Arizona, Utah, and Colorado.</p>
<p>The Rose Bowl was exclusively a Big Ten-Pac-10 affair for 52 years, from 1946 (<a href="/wiki/1947_Rose_Bowl" title="1947 Rose Bowl">1947 Rose Bowl</a>) through 1997 (<a href="/wiki/1998_Rose_Bowl" title="1998 Rose Bowl">1998 Rose Bowl</a>). While the Big Ten dominated the game in the late 1940s and 1950s, and the Pac-10 dominated during the 1970s and early 1980s, over the entire 52-year span, each conference won 26 games.</p>
<p>The BCS era covered sixteen games, starting with the <a href="/wiki/1999_Rose_Bowl" title="1999 Rose Bowl">1999 (85th) Rose Bowl</a> to the <a href="/wiki/2014_Rose_Bowl" title="2014 Rose Bowl">2014 (100th) Rose Bowl</a>. Of the eleven games featuring a Big Ten-Pac-12 matchup, the Pac-12 leads in wins, 7-4. However, the <a href="/wiki/2007_Rose_Bowl" title="2007 Rose Bowl">2007 (93rd) Rose Bowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008_Rose_Bowl" title="2008 Rose Bowl">2008 (94th) Rose Bowl</a>, each Big Ten losses to the Pac-12, actually featured the Big Ten runner-up, as conference champion Ohio State was selected to play each of those years in the <a href="/wiki/BCS_National_Championship_Game" title="BCS National Championship Game">BCS National Championship Game</a>.</p>
<p>The 2002 and 2006 Rose Bowls represented the National Championship game. In 2002, there was neither a Big Ten or Pac-10 school, as Oregon was in the Fiesta Bowl while Illinois was in the Sugar Bowl. In 2006, top-ranked USC represented the Pac-10 in the National Championship Game in the Rose Bowl against second-ranked Texas from the Big 12 Conference. Third-ranked Penn State, the Big Ten Champion, played in the Orange Bowl while fourth-ranked Ohio State played in the Fiesta Bowl that year.</p>
<p>The other two non-Big Ten vs. Pac-10 matchups in the BCS era involved Big 12 Conference teams: Oklahoma in the <a href="/wiki/2003_Rose_Bowl" title="2003 Rose Bowl">2003 (89th) Rose Bowl</a> and Texas in the <a href="/wiki/2005_Rose_Bowl" title="2005 Rose Bowl">2005 (91st) Rose Bowl</a>.</p>
<p>The 2011 Rose Bowl Game marked the sole time during the BCS era that a school from a non-BCS conference played in the game (as Oregon, the Pacific-10 champion, was selected to play in the <a href="/wiki/BCS_National_Championship_Game" title="BCS National Championship Game">BCS National Championship Game</a>). TCU, led by Quarterback Andy Dalton, beat Wisconsin 21–19.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Game_arrangements">Game arrangements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Game arrangements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg/220px-2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg/330px-2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg/440px-2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2006_Rose_Bowl_go-ahead_touchdown.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/2006_Rose_Bowl" title="2006 Rose Bowl">2006 Rose Bowl</a>: Texas defeated Southern California 41–38 on January 4, 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/1947_Rose_Bowl" title="1947 Rose Bowl">1947 Rose Bowl</a>, the Pacific Coast representative was the home team, and the Big Nine representative was the visitor. This arrangement would alternate each year. The stadium seating started with the Big Nine representatives in the end zone, but eventually was set with the Big Ten fans and team on the West (press box) side, and Pacific-10 fans and team on the East side. The home team wears their darkest home jerseys, and the visiting team wears the white visiting jerseys. There have been exceptions to the uniform arrangement: UCLA wore their home jerseys, light blue, in the 1962, 1966, and 1976 Rose Bowl games, with the Big Ten opponent also wearing their home uniforms.</p>
<p>From 1947 through 2001, the Big Ten team was the home team in odd-numbered years, and the Pac-10 team was the home team in even-numbered years. In 2003, Washington State was the home team, as a non-Big Ten or Pac-10 school (Oklahoma of the Big 12) was the opponent; the same applied in 2005, when Michigan played another Big 12 school, Texas.</p>
<p>Beginning with the 2002 Rose Bowl, Nebraska was home, with team and fans on the East sideline. From 2006 through 2013, the home team has been the team with the highest BCS season ending ranking. For the 2005 Rose Bowl, the Michigan team was on the East sideline; Texas was the visiting team and was on the West sideline. For the 2006 Rose Bowl, USC was the home team and Texas was the visiting team on the West sideline. Traditionally, the Big Ten (or its BCS replacement) is on the West side (press box) and the Pac-12 team is on the East side.</p>
<p>During the BCS era, the institution with the higher BCS ranking performed the national anthem, and performed first at halftime. With the exception of BCS championship years, the National Anthem was performed by the band. In BCS Championship years, a performer was invited to sing the Anthem, the last being <a href="/wiki/LeAnn_Rimes" title="LeAnn Rimes">LeAnn Rimes</a> in 2006. The Rose Bowl does not have other performers during the halftime show besides the school marching bands. As part of the television contract, a portion of each band's halftime performance is shown on television. Each school and each conference are allocated television spots to advertise. For the 100th game on January 1, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Merry_Clayton" title="Merry Clayton">Merry Clayton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Fischer" title="Lisa Fischer">Lisa Fischer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judith_Hill" title="Judith Hill">Judith Hill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darlene_Love" title="Darlene Love">Darlene Love</a> sang the national anthem. This was the first time in Rose Bowl history that the anthem was performed by singers rather than by a marching band.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> Today, the institution with the higher ranking by the CFP selection committee performs the national anthem and performs first at halftime.</p>

<p><i>^ The Pacific Coast Conference, predecessor to the Pac-12 Conference, had their first football season in 1916, so Washington State's appearance in 1916 for the 1915 season and Stanford's appearance in 1902 for the 1901 season do not count toward the conference's total.</i></p>
<p><i>* Penn State was not a member of the Big Ten at the time of their 1923 Rose Bowl appearance, so Penn State's appearance in 1923 for the 1922 season does not count toward the Big Ten's conference's total</i></p>
<p><i>† Nebraska was not a member of the Big Ten at the time of either of their appearances. Therefore, as of 2017, they have not represented the Big Ten in the Rose Bowl.</i></p>
<p><i>‡ The Southeastern Conference has one current member and two former members who made additional appearances in the Rose Bowl while those schools were not members of the SEC. Alabama made additional appearances in 1926, 1927, and 1931 before becoming a charter member of the SEC in 1932. Another SEC charter member, Georgia Tech, made an appearance in 1929, and left the SEC in 1964. Tulane, also a charter member, made an appearance in 1932, prior to the SEC's establishment in December of that year. Tulane left the SEC in 1966.</i></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rose_Bowl_Most_Valuable_Player_Award">Rose Bowl Most Valuable Player Award</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rose Bowl Most Valuable Player Award">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg/220px-UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg/330px-UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg/440px-UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UCLA_Rose_Bowl_record.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rose Bowl records at the Hall of Champions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most valuable player in the Rose Bowl game is given a crystal trophy that is the <b>Rose Bowl Most Valuable Player Award</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> The award was created in 1953 and awarded retroactively for players all the way back to the <a href="/wiki/1902_Rose_Bowl" title="1902 Rose Bowl">1902 Rose Bowl</a>. Occasionally, the award has been shared by two players. Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/2005_Rose_Bowl" title="2005 Rose Bowl">2005 Rose Bowl</a> Game, the Rose Bowl MVP Award has been given to both an offensive and defensive player.<sup id="cite_ref-ROSEBOWLPROGRAM_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ROSEBOWLPROGRAM-53">[49]</a></sup> Four players have been named the MVP of more than one Rose Bowl: <a href="/wiki/Bob_Schloredt" title="Bob Schloredt">Bob Schloredt</a>, Washington (1960, 1961), <a href="/wiki/Charles_White_(American_football)" title="Charles White (American football)">Charles White</a>, USC (1979, 1980), <a href="/wiki/Ron_Dayne" title="Ron Dayne">Ron Dayne</a>, Wisconsin (1999, 2000), and <a href="/wiki/Vince_Young" title="Vince Young">Vince Young</a>, Texas (2005, 2006).</p>

<h3><span id="No._1–ranked_teams"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="No._1.E2.80.93ranked_teams">No. 1–ranked teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: No. 1–ranked teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>1954 Season/1955 Game: No. 1 Ohio State defeated No. 17 USC, 20–7</li>
<li>1960 Season/1961 Game: No. 6 Washington defeated No. 1 Minnesota, 17–7</li>
<li>1962 Season/1963 Game: No. 1 USC defeated No. 2 Wisconsin, 42–37</li>
<li>1965 Season/1966 Game: No. 5 UCLA defeated No. 1 Michigan State, 14–12</li>
<li>1968 Season/1969 Game: No. 1 Ohio State defeated No. 2 USC, 27–16</li>
<li>1972 Season/1973 Game: No. 1 USC defeated No. 3 Ohio State, 42-17</li>
<li>1975 Season/1976 Game: No. 11 UCLA defeated No. 1 Ohio State, 23-10</li>
<li>1979 Season/1980 Game: No. 3 USC defeated No. 1 Ohio State, 17–16</li>
<li>1997 Season/1998 Game: No. 1 Michigan defeated No. 8 Washington State, 21–16</li>
<li>2001 Season/2002 BCS National Championship Game: No. 1 Miami defeated No. 4 Nebraska, 37–14</li>
<li>2003 Season/2004 Game: No. 1 USC defeated No. 4 Michigan, 28–14</li>
<li>2005 Season/2006 BCS National Championship Game: No. 2 Texas defeated No. 1 USC, 41–38</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No._1_vs._No._2_teams">No. 1 vs. No. 2 teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: No. 1 vs. No. 2 teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>1962 Season/1963 Game: No. 1 USC defeated No. 2 Wisconsin, 42–37.</li>
<li>1968 Season/1969 Game: No. 1 Ohio State defeated No. 2 USC, 27–16. Ohio State was voted national champion.</li>
<li>2005 Season/2006 BCS National Championship Game: No. 2 Texas defeated No. 1 USC, 41–38. Texas was voted national champion.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Twice_in_a_season">Twice in a season</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Twice in a season">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Five times in Rose Bowl Game history, the two participants had played during the regular season. In three of those instances, the same team won both the regular season game and the Rose Bowl Game. UCLA won three of those five Rose Bowl games, including both instances in which a different team lost the regular season game but won the Rose Bowl Game.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>1956</b> <a href="/wiki/1956_Iowa_Hawkeyes_football_team" title="1956 Iowa Hawkeyes football team">Iowa</a> 14, <a href="/wiki/1956_Oregon_State_Beavers_football_team" title="1956 Oregon State Beavers football team">Oregon State</a> 13; <a href="/wiki/1957_Rose_Bowl" title="1957 Rose Bowl">1957 Rose Bowl</a> Rematch: Iowa 35, Oregon State 19</li>
<li><b>1965</b> <a href="/wiki/1965_Michigan_State_Spartans_football_team" title="1965 Michigan State Spartans football team">Michigan State</a> 13, <a href="/wiki/1965_UCLA_Bruins_football_team" title="1965 UCLA Bruins football team">UCLA</a> 3; <a href="/wiki/1966_Rose_Bowl" title="1966 Rose Bowl">1966 Rose Bowl</a> Rematch: UCLA 14, Michigan State 12</li>
<li><b>1975</b> <a href="/wiki/1975_Ohio_State_Buckeyes_football_team" title="1975 Ohio State Buckeyes football team">Ohio State</a> 41, <a href="/wiki/1975_UCLA_Bruins_football_team" title="1975 UCLA Bruins football team">UCLA</a> 20; <a href="/wiki/1976_Rose_Bowl" title="1976 Rose Bowl">1976 Rose Bowl</a> Rematch: UCLA 23, Ohio State 10</li>
<li><b>1982</b> <a href="/wiki/1982_UCLA_Bruins_football_team" title="1982 UCLA Bruins football team">UCLA</a> 31, <a href="/wiki/1982_Michigan_Wolverines_football_team" title="1982 Michigan Wolverines football team">Michigan</a> 27; <a href="/wiki/1983_Rose_Bowl" title="1983 Rose Bowl">1983 Rose Bowl</a> Rematch: UCLA 24, Michigan 14</li>
<li><b>1987</b> <a href="/wiki/1987_Michigan_State_Spartans_football_team" title="1987 Michigan State Spartans football team">Michigan State</a> 27, <a href="/wiki/1987_USC_Trojans_football_team" title="1987 USC Trojans football team">USC</a> 13; <a href="/wiki/1988_Rose_Bowl" title="1988 Rose Bowl">1988 Rose Bowl</a> Rematch: Michigan State 20, USC 17</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rose_Bowl_Hall_of_Fame">Rose Bowl Hall of Fame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rose Bowl Hall of Fame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><b>2015</b> – <a href="/wiki/Mark_Brunell" title="Mark Brunell">Mark Brunell</a>, Washington; Jim Muldoon (Pac-10); <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Pollard" title="Fritz Pollard">Fritz Pollard</a>, Brown; and <a href="/wiki/Tyrone_Wheatley" title="Tyrone Wheatley">Tyrone Wheatley</a>, Michigan<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>2016</b> – <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Bell" title="Bobby Bell">Bobby Bell</a>, Minnesota; <a href="/wiki/Ricky_Ervins" title="Ricky Ervins">Ricky Ervins</a>, USC; <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Prothro" title="Tommy Prothro">Tommy Prothro</a>, UCLA and <a href="/wiki/Art_Spander" title="Art Spander">Art Spander</a>, UCLA<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>2017</b> – <a href="/wiki/Mack_Brown" title="Mack Brown">Mack Brown</a>, Texas; <a href="/wiki/Cade_McNown" title="Cade McNown">Cade McNown</a>, UCLA; <a href="/wiki/Charles_Woodson" title="Charles Woodson">Charles Woodson</a>, Michigan; and Dr. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fremont_West" title="Charles Fremont West">Charles West</a>, Washington &amp; Jefferson</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="All-Century_Class">All-Century Class</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: All-Century Class">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Rose Bowl Game All-Century Class was announced by Rose Bowl Hall of Fame member Keith Jackson, on Sunday, December 8, 2013 at 4:00&#160;p.m. at Tournament House.</p>
<p>They are:</p>
<ul>
<li>1900s–1910s: <a href="/wiki/George_Halas" title="George Halas">George Halas</a> (Great Lakes Navy)</li>
<li>1920s: <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Nevers" title="Ernie Nevers">Ernie Nevers</a> (Stanford)</li>
<li>1930s: <a href="/wiki/Don_Hutson" title="Don Hutson">Don Hutson</a> (Alabama) and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Jones_(American_football_coach)" title="Howard Jones (American football coach)">Howard Jones</a> (USC)</li>
<li>1940s: <a href="/wiki/Charley_Trippi" title="Charley Trippi">Charley Trippi</a> (Georgia)</li>
<li>1950s: <a href="/wiki/Woody_Hayes" title="Woody Hayes">Woody Hayes</a> (Ohio State)</li>
<li>1960s: <a href="/wiki/John_McKay_(American_football)" title="John McKay (American football)">John McKay</a> (USC)</li>
<li>1970s: <a href="/wiki/Archie_Griffin" title="Archie Griffin">Archie Griffin</a> (Ohio State)</li>
<li>1980s: <a href="/wiki/Bo_Schembechler" title="Bo Schembechler">Bo Schembechler</a> (Michigan)</li>
<li>1990s: <a href="/wiki/Ron_Dayne" title="Ron Dayne">Ron Dayne</a> (Wisconsin)</li>
<li>2000s: <a href="/wiki/Vince_Young" title="Vince Young">Vince Young</a> (Texas)</li>
<li>2010s: <a href="/wiki/Montee_Ball" title="Montee Ball">Montee Ball</a> (Wisconsin)</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to being named as All-Century representatives for their respective decades, John McKay and Archie Griffin were named the 100th Rose Bowl Game All-Century Coach and Player respectively. Griffin and McKay (represented by his son <a href="/wiki/John_McKay,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="John McKay, Jr.">John McKay, Jr.</a>) will participate in the 2014 Rose Parade.</p>
<p>The finalists:</p>
<ul>
<li>1900–1919: <a href="/wiki/Paddy_Driscoll" title="Paddy Driscoll">Paddy Driscoll</a> (Great Lakes Navy, 1919), <a href="/wiki/Neil_Snow" title="Neil Snow">Neil Snow</a> (Michigan, 1902) and George Halas (Great Lakes Navy, 1919)</li>
<li>1920–1929: Ernie Nevers (Stanford, 1925), <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Layden" title="Elmer Layden">Elmer Layden</a> (Notre Dame, 1925) and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mack_Brown" title="Johnny Mack Brown">Johnny Mack Brown</a> (Alabama, 1926)</li>
<li>1930–1939: <a href="/wiki/Millard_%22Dixie%22_Howell" class="mw-redirect" title="Millard &quot;Dixie&quot; Howell">Millard "Dixie" Howell</a> (Alabama, 1935), Don Hutson (Alabama, 1935) and Howard Jones (USC, 1930, 1932–33, 1939–40)</li>
<li>1940–1949: <a href="/wiki/Bob_Chappuis" title="Bob Chappuis">Bob Chappuis</a> (Michigan, 1948), <a href="/wiki/Harry_Gilmer" title="Harry Gilmer">Harry Gilmer</a> (Alabama, 1946) and Charley Trippi (Georgia, 1943)</li>
<li>1950–1959: <a href="/wiki/Alan_Ameche" title="Alan Ameche">Alan Ameche</a> (Wisconsin, 1953), <a href="/wiki/Bob_Jeter" title="Bob Jeter">Bob Jeter</a> (Iowa, 1959) and Woody Hayes (Ohio State, 1954, 1957, 1968, 1970, 1972–1975)</li>
<li>1960–1969: <a href="/wiki/Ron_Vander_Kelen" title="Ron Vander Kelen">Ron Vander Kelen</a> (Wisconsin, 1963), <a href="/wiki/O.J._Simpson" class="mw-redirect" title="O.J. Simpson">O.J. Simpson</a> (USC, 1968-69) and John McKay (USC, 1963, 1967–70, 1973–1975)</li>
<li>1970–1979: <a href="/wiki/Jim_Plunkett" title="Jim Plunkett">Jim Plunkett</a> (Stanford, 1971), <a href="/wiki/Charles_White_(American_football)" title="Charles White (American football)">Charles White</a> (USC, 1979–1980) and Archie Griffin (Ohio State, 1973–1976)</li>
<li>1980–1989: <a href="/wiki/Don_James_(American_football)" title="Don James (American football)">Don James</a> (Washington, 1978, 1981–82, 1991–93), <a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(American_football_coach)" title="John Robinson (American football coach)">John Robinson</a> (USC, 1977, 1979–80, 1996) and Bo Schembechler (Michigan, 1970, 1972, 1977–79, 1981, 1983, 1987, 1989–90)</li>
<li>1990–1999: <a href="/wiki/Barry_Alvarez" title="Barry Alvarez">Barry Alvarez</a> (Wisconsin, 1994, 1999, 2000 and 2013), <a href="/wiki/Keyshawn_Johnson" title="Keyshawn Johnson">Keyshawn Johnson</a> (USC, 1996) and Ron Dayne (Wisconsin, 1999 and 2000)</li>
<li>2000–2009: <a href="/wiki/Matt_Leinart" title="Matt Leinart">Matt Leinart</a> (USC, 2004 and 2006), Vince Young (Texas, 2005–06) and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Cushing" title="Brian Cushing">Brian Cushing</a> (USC, 2006–09), John David Booty (USC 2006–07)</li>
<li>2010–2012: <a href="/wiki/Terrelle_Pryor" title="Terrelle Pryor">Terrelle Pryor</a> (Ohio State, 2010), <a href="/wiki/Tank_Carder" title="Tank Carder">Tank Carder</a> (TCU, 2011) and Montee Ball (Wisconsin, 2011–13)</li>
</ul>
<p>Nominated by <i><a href="/wiki/Football_Writers_Association_of_America" title="Football Writers Association of America">The Football Writers Association of America</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Player_and_coach">Player and coach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Player and coach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nine former players have come back to coach a team in the game (played and coached, as listed by the Tournament of Roses Association):<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Bret Bielema: Iowa (1991); Wisconsin (2011, 2012)</li>
<li>Terry Donahue: UCLA (1966); UCLA (1983, 1984, 1986, 1994)</li>
<li>Bump Elliott: Michigan (1948); Michigan (1965)</li>
<li>Pete Elliott: Michigan (1948); California (1959); Illinois (1964)</li>
<li>Jess Hill: USC (1930); USC (1953, 1955)</li>
<li>Shy Huntington: Oregon (1917); Oregon (1920)</li>
<li>Rick Neuheisel: UCLA (1983, 1984 – MVP); Washington (2001)</li>
<li>John Robinson: Oregon (1958); USC (1977, 1979, 1980, 1996)</li>
<li>Chuck Taylor: Stanford (1941); Stanford (1952)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coaches_with_two_teams">Coaches with two teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Coaches with two teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Hugo Bezdek: Oregon, 1917; Mare Island, 1918; Penn State, 1923 (three teams)</li>
<li>John Cooper: Arizona State, 1987; Ohio State, 1997 (Only coach to win the Rose Bowl Game with both a Big Ten and Pac-10 team)</li>
<li>Bill “Lone Star” Dietz: Washington State, 1916; Mare Island, 1919</li>
<li>Pete Elliott: California, 1959; Illinois, 1964</li>
<li>Robert Folwell: Pennsylvania, 1917; Navy 1924</li>
<li>Tommy Prothro: Oregon State, 1965; UCLA 1966</li>
<li>Wallace Wade: Alabama, 1926, 1927, 1931; Duke 1939, 1942</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related">Related</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Related">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Events">Events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Selection Sunday, December 6, 2015<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lawry%27s" title="Lawry's">Lawry's</a> Beef Bowl – December 27, 28, 2015</li>
<li>Hall of Fame ceremony, Pasadena Convention Center, December 30, 2015</li>
<li>Kickoff Luncheon, Rose Bowl, December 31, 2015</li>
<li>Rose Bowl Game Public Tailgate, January 1, 2016</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rose_Bowl_Game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>January 1, 1918 – <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Bezdek" title="Hugo Bezdek">Hugo Bezdek</a> was the first college football coach to lead two separate schools (Oregon, 1917 and Mare Island, 1918) to victories; He also coached Penn State in a loss in the 1923 Rose Bowl</li>
<li>January 2, 1922 – First bowl game not played on New Year's Day</li>
<li>January 3, 2002 – First time hosting the <a href="/wiki/BCS_National_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="BCS National Championship">BCS National Championship</a> where <a href="/wiki/2001_Miami_Hurricanes_football_team" title="2001 Miami Hurricanes football team">Miami</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/2001_Nebraska_Cornhuskers_football_team" title="2001 Nebraska Cornhuskers football team">Nebraska</a>, 37-14, and the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Parade" title="Rose Parade">Rose Parade</a> and the Rose Bowl Game were held on separate days</li>
</ul>

<li>Pacific-10 Conference football media guide. (PDF copy available at <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.pac-10.org">http://www.pac-10.org</a>.)</li>

<tr>
<th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="background:#ED1C24; color:#FFFFFF; border:2px solid #008348;;width:1%">Notes</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li>No games were held from 1903 to 1915.</li>
<li>The 1942 game was played at <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Wade_Stadium" title="Wallace Wade Stadium">Duke Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_North_Carolina" title="Durham, North Carolina">Durham, NC</a>, due to the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>.</li>
<li>The game was also the national title game for the <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">Bowl Championship Series</a> in 2002 and 2006.</li>
<li>The game was a <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Playoff" title="College Football Playoff">College Football Playoff</a> semifinal in its 2015 edition.</li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
